        "metadata": {
            "data_product_name": "Raw Beef Product Sampling - Sample for Updated Product Laboratory Sampling Datasets",
            "description": "The Lab Sampling datasets contain relevant information pertaining to USDA lab sampling projects and tasks. The data include, but are not limited to, identifying establishment information, the sampling project, and results from the samples taken. The sampling results are grouped into seven pathogens: generic E. coli, E. coli O157:H7, Shiga toxin-producing E. coli (STEC), Salmonella, Campylobacter, Listeria, and Enterococcus. Samples can be identified by the unique identifiers known as the form_id and sample_number. Each form_id corresponds to an entire sample, while the sample_number corresponds to various tests that can be performed on the sample. ",
            "lab_sampling_tests_structure": "Each form_id can have one or multiple sample_number values. The sampling tests and their sample_numbers are structured in a primary-secondary relationship. In most cases, a 'primary' test will be performed first, and a 'secondary' test will be performed based on the results of the primary test. For example, a primary test would be if a sample is tested for the presence of Salmonella. If the result is Negative, this primary test will not have an associated secondary test. If the result is Positive, however, a secondary test will be performed to determine the serotype of the Salmonella isolate. Finally, a 'tertiary' test can be performed as well. Furthering the previous example, once the Salmonella isolate is determined, the allele code will then be entered for that isolate. In this example, the secondary test is dependent upon the primary test, and the tertiary test is dependent upon the secondary test. This may not always be the case as there do exist 'orphan' secondary/tertiary tests. In most cases, these can be explained by third parties who conduct sampling projects on behalf of the USDA which may not perform the primary tests, or the primary sample data is not received by the USDA.",
            "disclaimer": "Data postings are part of the Agency's efforts to prevent pathogens from entering the food supply throughout the farm-to-fork continuum. Posting these datasets may help industry identify repetitive subtypes and implement control measures. It may allow researchers to identify trends to address basic research questions or to develop new diagnostics or therapies, such as vaccines. \n These datasets are posted for informational purposes only and are not sufficient to determine if there is an association between multiple samples. Therefore, this data should not be used to identify foodborne illness outbreaks, associate samples with foodborne illness outbreaks or determine whether two or more samples are causally related. Further epidemiologic information is needed to determine if there is an association among the non-clinical isolates and human illnesses. ",
            "json_file_format": "This dataset is available in an open, non-proprietary, publicly accessible JavaScript Object Notation (JSON) format consistent with requirements set by the Foundations for Evidence-Based Policymaking Act of 2018 (https://www.congress.gov/115/plaws/publ435/PLAW-115publ435.pdf). Additional information can be found on the FSIS Laboratory Sampling Data web page (https://www.fsis.usda.gov/science-data/data-sets-visualizations/laboratory-sampling-data).",
            "tidy_data_format": "The Lab Sampling datasets will adhere to tidy data principles. Tidy data is a framework for standardizing a dataset which maps its meaning to its structure: each variable forms a column, each observation forms a row, each value forms a cell, and each observational unit forms a dataset. One of the major efforts in tidying the Lab Sampling data included expanding sampling tests into unique columns. Doing so allows the data to adhere to the aforementioned tidy data principles: each row is a unique sample_number, each column is a test that is performed on the sample, and each cell is the result of that test, if performed. More information pertaining to tidy data principles can be found here: https://www.jstatsoft.org/article/view/v059i10. \n Additionally, to maintain consistency across datasets, FSIS will begin utilizing 'snake_case' for all variable names. This format consists of all lowercase letters, and every space is replaced by an underscore. Some examples include: establishment_id, establishment_name, salmonella_serotype, allele_code, etc. \n Data will be broken out by fiscal year (FY), starting with data from FY14 (starting on October 1, 2013) through the most recent FY. Files will be updated on a quarterly basis. ",
            "primary_secondary_data_products": "In rare cases, a single secondary test can be performed multiple times. This occurs when multiple Salmonella serotypes are identified from a single positive Salmonella sample. If this was placed into a single dataset, this would violate tidy data principles as this would cause two rows to have the same primary sample_number value (the same observation in multiple rows). To fix this, the datasets were split into a primary dataset and a secondary dataset. The primary dataset contains only the primary test information, one per row. The secondary dataset contains all the secondary test information, one per row. The secondary dataset also contains all tertiary test information because in no cases can a single tertiary test be performed multiple times, so the secondary and tertiary information can safely be placed in the same row with no issues. ",
            "joining_primary_secondary_data_products": "In order to properly join the primary and secondary datasets to see all available data, use the column form_id as the primary/foreign key, and utilize a full outer join (sometimes simply called an outer join). A full outer join is needed to ensure all orphan secondary samples are also included in the final result. For example, a simple SQL query would look like this: \n <  SELECT * FROM pathogen_primary_test ppt FULL JOIN pathogen_secondary_test pct ON pct.form_id = ppt.form_id  > \n The pathogen_primary_test dataset also includes the Establishment ID from the establishment where the sample was taken. Use this variable (in this dataset named 'establishment_id' with lowercase letters and an underscore) to join the pathogen_primary_test dataset to other FSIS datasets. ",
            "source": "PHIS",
            "creation_date": "July 31, 2024",

            "fsis_number_wgs_information": "The FSIS number is a unique identifier for retrieving whole genome sequence data from the National Center for Biotechnology and Information (NCBI) Pathogen Detection Isolates Browser (https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pathogens/isolates/). The allele codes included in this dataset provide a convenient naming method for reporting Whole Genome Sequencing (WGS) data. Because allele codes can change over time as more WGS data becomes available, a date stamp allows the data to be used in reports. Due to the increased discriminatory power of WGS, FSIS transitioned away from Pulsed-Field Gel Electrophoresis (PFGE) to using WGS as the typing tool in 2019 along with CDC and public health partners. The PFGE data is included for transparency purposes and is not intended to be used in any investigative processes through any data releases. ",
            "outbreak_information": "FSIS, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) and other public health partners monitor WGS information gathered from samples in real time and use sophisticated epidemiological tools to identify whether the cumulative findings might signal a foodborne illness outbreak. Outbreaks identified through this process are announced to the public through CDC's website and FSIS' Outbreak Response Page (https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pathogens/isolates/). The FSIS investigative process is described in FSIS Directive 8080.3 (https://www.fsis.usda.gov/policy/fsis-directives/8080.3).  ",

            "data_product_overview": "Prior to February 1, 2023, samples that have a project code of MT43, MT54, MT55, MT64, and MT65 were only tested for E. coli O157:H7. Therefore, samples received prior to February 1, 2023 will have NULL values in the fields for stec_[O26, O45, O103, O111, O121, O145]_serogroup if the test was not performed. \n FSIS switched to the Cloth Sampling Method for the following project codes in February 2023: MT44T, MT52, MT53, MT60, MT65. Those project codes were retired and replaced with the following project codes: MT44T_C, MT52_C, MT53_C, MT60_C, and MT65_C. For positive samples detected before February 2023, FSIS assigned a follow-up sampling set using the N60 methodology (sample tasks with no '_C' in the project code, MT52, MT53, or MT44T), even if some of those samples were planned to be collected after February 2023. However, if a positive sample is detected after February 2023, FSIS will assign a follow-up sampling set using the cloth sampling methodology ('C' in the project code MT52_C, MT53_C, or MT44T_C). For more information, please see FSIS Directive 10010.1. \n FSIS did not conduct Salmonella analysis for the MT55 and MT60 sampling projects prior to July 2014. \n Prior analysis using this data can be found on the FSIS website, such as the Microbiological Testing Program for Escherichia coli O157:H7 and non-O157 Shiga toxin-producing Escherichia coli (STEC) (https://www.fsis.usda.gov/science-data/data-sets-visualizations/microbiology/microbiological-testing-program-escherichia-coli) web page.",
            "notes_limitations": "Information about FSIS sampling laboratories and procedures can be found on the FSIS website on the Laboratories & Procedures (https://www.fsis.usda.gov/science-data/laboratories-procedures) web page and the Microbiology Laboratory Guidebook (https://www.fsis.usda.gov/news-events/publications/microbiology-laboratory-guidebook) (MLG) web page. \n NULL values indicate that the specific variable is not available for that record. \n Samples analyzed for Salmonella and Campylobacter may lack results for one organism. Lack of analysis for one organism is due to either insufficient volume to perform both analyses or not being analyzed due to not meeting all receipt temperature criteria; MLG Chapter 1.01 (https://www.fsis.usda.gov/sites/default/files/media_file/2022-03/MLG_1.01.pdf). Delays in sample analysis can occur when a sample is non-viable or when there is a need to repeat the WGS analysis. \n When a sample screens positive for a pathogen, there normally is only one isolate (e.g., subtype determined using serology [serotype], PFGE, antibiotic resistance, allele code, or whole genome sequence) derived from laboratory confirmation procedures. During such procedures, the enrichment broth is streaked on agar plates, and those plates are subsequently examined for typical pathogen colonies. The laboratory staff ordinarily picks no more than one typical isolated colony from any one plate. On very rare occasions, more than one typical colony may be picked for confirmation. In such circumstances, the multiple isolate data (e.g., Salmonella serotype) are separated by a semicolon. \n Confirmed positive bacterial isolates can become unsuitable for additional testing during shipping, transfer, or storage. This result is final as further characterization cannot be performed. When this occurs, the isolate characterization result fields (e.g., serotype, antimicrobial resistance, allele code) will be populated with 'Final result - characterization not available' and the FSIS Number will be reported as 'FSISNOTSEQ'. \n WGS data must be interpreted within the context of how it will be used, e.g., to detect outbreaks or contamination events. Additional corroborating information, including case-patient food exposure and product distribution records, may be necessary to properly interpret the WGS data.",
